# Chapter 1

## **CHAPTER 1**

The deer was dead, but the drone was watching.

I knelt in the wet duff of the forest floor, my fingers on the doe’s still-warm neck, feeling for the pulse that wasn’t there. My own breath plumed in the November air, a small, persistent ghost. The arrow had done its work clean, high through the lungs. She’d run maybe forty yards, a heart-shot sprint, before folding into this cradle of ferns and rotten hemlock. Good. A good death. A respectful one. I’d thanked her spirit, my words a low murmur lost in the drip of moisture from cedar boughs. I’d laid my tobacco tie in the crook of her foreleg. The ritual was complete, the old contract honored.

Then I heard it. Not a natural sound. A mechanical mosquito whine, thin and persistent, slicing through the forest’s low hum.

I didn’t look up. Looking up is what they want. I kept my hands on the deer, my head bowed, but my eyes scanned the canopy. There. A sliver of shadow against the grey wool of the sky, hovering maybe sixty feet up, between two towering Douglas firs. A quadcopter. Sleek, matte black, no markings. It wasn’t moving. It was observing.

“*K’yats’*,” I whispered. Go away.

It didn’t go away. It adjusted its position with a minute, precise buzz, lens glinting like a dead, black eye.

Anger, hot and sudden, tightened my throat. This was the deep woods, ten miles from the nearest logging road, on our Reserve’s protected uplands. This was *our* place. My uncle’s trapline. My grandfather’s hunting grounds. This quiet, this privacy, was the last thin layer of insulation we had left. And now this thing, this silent, hovering sentry, was peeling it back.

I stood up slowly, wiping my hands on my canvas pants. I faced the drone. Raised my middle finger to it, a slow, deliberate gesture my grandfather would have scoffed at. “*A’nó:wara*,” I said, louder. “You are not welcome here.”

For a few seconds, nothing. Just the whine and the watching. Then, as if satisfied, it pivoted and zipped away north, silent as a ghost, leaving only the memory of its violation in the air.

The peace was gone. The harvest was now just meat. The ritual felt like a performance.

I worked quickly, field-dressing the doe with the practiced, efficient movements my uncle Tobin had drilled into me since I was twelve. The steam rose from the body cavity, rich and metallic. My hands were warm with blood. I tried to sink back into the mindfulness of the task, the gratitude, but my ears were pricked for that whine. Every chickadee’s scold sounded like a rotor start-up.

By the time I had her trussed and draped over my shoulders in a fireman’s carry, the light was leaching from the sky. The walk back to my truck was two miles, all downhill. The weight was good, honest. My boots sank into the moss. But my mind was a swarm.

It was the third one this month. The first, I’d mistaken for a raven until it banked in a way no bird ever does. The second had been higher, a speck following the river corridor. This one was brazen. This one wanted to be seen.

I hit the old skid road, my truck a dark green lump in the gathering twilight. As I heaved the deer into the bed, covering her with a tarp, I saw the sticker on the bumper of the parked white SUV I hadn’t noticed before: a stylized pine tree overlaid with a circuit board, and the words “TERRA-VISION INC. – Ecological Futures.”

Ecological Futures. My ass.

I was wiping my hands on a rag when he emerged from the trees. City shoes. Khaki pants, a Patagonia vest over a checked shirt. Mid-forties, clean-shaven, a smile that didn’t reach his eyes. He held a tablet.

“Elara Bear?” he said, as if he wasn’t sure. He was sure.

“Who’s asking?”

“Mark Devlin. Terra-Vision. We have a permit from the Band Council for land survey.” He didn’t offer to shake my bloody hand. Smart.

“Survey for what?”

“Biodiversity mapping. Non-invasive habitat assessment. It’s part of the new Shared Stewardship Accord with the province.” He said it like he was reading a press release. He probably was. “We’re deploying next-gen sensor arrays—acoustic, visual, thermal. Building a real-time model of the ecosystem’s health.”

“You mean drones.”

“Uncrewed Aerial Systems, yes. For a holistic picture.”

I looked past him, into the woods where I’d killed the deer. “Holistic. You watch me take that doe?”

He blinked, the smile faltering for a second. “Our systems record aggregate data points for wildlife movement and behavior. Individual human activity is… ancillary, unless it presents a conservation risk.”

“Ancillary.” I let the word hang. “You flew your *ancillary* eye right over my prayer. Over my harvest.”

“Ms. Bear, I assure you, there’s no profiling intent. This is about science. About protecting all this.” He gestured broadly at the forest, which was now just a wall of darkness. “Your community leadership is fully onboard. This technology can help enforce hunting quotas, track species recovery…”

“We know our quotas. We know the recovery. My uncle knows every fisher den in these hills. My aunt knows every medicinal root that’s gone missing. We don’t need your black flies to tell us what we already hear.”

He sighed, a patient, put-upon sound. “The Accord is signed. The partnership is active. We’re here to help.”

“We didn’t ask for your help.”

“Your Council did.” He tapped his tablet, and it glowed, a little square of artificial day. “The world is changing, Ms. Bear. Data is the new currency of care. You can’t protect what you can’t measure.”

I turned my back on him, threw my gear into the passenger seat. The old truck door groaned when I opened it. “We’ve been measuring for ten thousand years. We use different math.”

I got in. He stood there, illuminated by my headlights. Just before I turned the ignition, he leaned down slightly.

“That’s a beautiful animal. You should register the harvest in the app. It’ll help calibrate our models.”

I drove away. In my rearview, he was just a shrinking white figure, holding his little window of light.

***

The road home was a black ribbon. My headlights carved a tunnel through the cedars. My hands were still faintly sticky on the wheel.

*Data is the new currency of care.*

My grandfather, *Aronhiaies*, would have spat at the words. Care was a song for the salmon’s return. Care was leaving the last berry on the bush. Care was a relationship, a reciprocity. It was not a currency. It was not extracted, stored, and sold.

But my grandfather was five years in the ground. And my older sister, Kaya, who sat on the Band Council, had a different mantra: *If we don’t own the story, someone else will*. She saw Terra-Vision, the Accords, the whole digital stewardship wave, as a tool. A dangerous one, but a tool nonetheless. “We get the data, Elara,” she’d argued over frybread last week. “We get the training, the jobs. We build the platform *with* our laws, our stories embedded. Otherwise, it’s just another colonial extraction, but faster and with better PR.”

“It *is* extraction,” I’d mumbled through a mouthful. “You’re just letting them use a different shovel.”

“It’s the only shovel left,” she’d said, her voice tired. “The province is bankrupt. They’re outsourcing ‘conservation’ to tech firms. We can be the outsourced labor, or we can be the partners. I choose partner.”

I swung the truck onto the long driveway that led to our family’s houses. My place was the old cabin, rebuilt and rewired, tucked behind my mother’s bigger home. Kaya lived in town, near the Band office. My uncle Tobin’s place was further back, against the treeline.

I saw the glow before I saw the house. A cool, blue-white light from my front porch. Not the yellow bug light I’d left on.

I parked, killed the engine. Sat in the dark for a minute. The drone’s whine seemed to have taken up residence in my inner ear.

On my porch, sitting neatly on the weathered plank floor, was a package. A cube, about the size of a shoebox, made of smooth, off-white biodegradable polymer. It had no label, no address. Just a single, small symbol etched into its top: a pine tree over a circuit board.

Terra-Vision.

I picked it up. It was light. I carried it inside, dumped it on my kitchen table. For a long time, I just looked at it, this sterile, alien artifact on my wooden table, next to a bowl of pinecones and a half-finished cup of tea.

It wasn’t locked. A simple latch released. The lid lifted silently.

Inside, nestled in molded pulp, was a headset. Not bulky VR goggles, but something sleek, minimalist—a curved band of brushed metal and dark glass. Beside it lay a single sheet of “stone paper,” the waterproof, mineral-based stuff. A handwritten note, in precise, elegant script.

*Elara—*

*A gesture of goodwill, and an invitation. This is a prototype. We call it the Prism. It doesn’t just show you data; it lets you see through the layers of the living world. The health of the soil. The history in the growth rings of a tree. The path of last winter’s wolves. It translates.*

*Your Council has seen its potential. We believe you, with your deep, lived knowledge of this territory, could help us… calibrate it. Help us ensure its translations are true.*

*The forest is speaking. This is just a new way to listen.*

*—M.D.*

I laughed. A short, sharp bark that echoed in my quiet kitchen. They were offering me a translator. For my own land. After their drone had just deafened me to the most important conversation I have—the one between my spirit and the spirit of the life I take.

I almost threw it in the woodstove. I almost took it out back and smashed it with a rock.

But I didn’t.

I left it on the table, this sleek, silent interrogator. I went outside, into the cold, clear night. I needed to wash the day off. I walked past my mother’s dark window, down to the little creek that bordered our property. The creek has a name, but it’s not for saying to outsiders. It’s a talking creek, all chuckles and whispers over smooth stones.

I knelt on the bank, plunged my hands into the water. It was so cold it burned. I scrubbed at the dried blood, the lingering feeling of the drone’s gaze, the smarmy words of Mark Devlin. The water swirled, dark, then cleared.

When I looked up, wiping my hands on my jacket, I saw a light.

Deep in the woods, on the far side of the creek, in a stand of old-growth hemlock that was supposed to be empty. A soft, greenish glow, pulsing gently, like a slow heartbeat. It wasn’t a flashlight. It wasn’t fire. It was a light that seemed to emanate from the tree trunk itself.

I froze. The creek’s chatter faded to a distant hum.

The glow pulsed again. Then, it began to move. Not like someone walking with a lamp, but drifting, weaving between the trees. A will-o’-the-wisp. A *jogah*, one of the little stone people of the old stories. My grandfather’s voice filled my head: *They show themselves when something is out of balance. When the agreements are broken.*

The green light hovered for a moment, as if looking back at me. Then it winked out, leaving only the profound, velvet black of the forest.

I stood there, shivering, my clean hands turning numb.

On one side of the creek: my world, with its dead deer, its watching drones, its translator-in-a-box.

On the other side: a ghost light from a story, signaling in a language I was terrified I might be forgetting how to understand.

The agreement *was* broken.

And I had a terrible feeling that the shiny tool on my kitchen table wasn’t going to help me fix it. It was just going to show me, in high definition, all the ways it was already shattered.

# Chapter 2

## **CHAPTER 2**

The headset sat on my table for three days. It became a part of the landscape, like the persistent knot in my shoulder from carrying the deer. I’d walk past it making coffee, and its sleek curves seemed to accuse me of cowardice. Or wisdom. I couldn’t tell which.

I avoided Kaya. I helped my mother split and stack wood for the winter, the repetitive thunk of the maul a better prayer than any words I could muster. I took the venison to my auntie’s, helped with the butchering, the wrapping, the rendering. Work that ended with a tangible result. You could hold it, freeze it, feed your family with it.

On the fourth morning, a different vehicle came up the drive. Not Mark Devlin’s white SUV, but my sister’s dusty Jeep Cherokee. She got out, her council-branded puffer jacket zipped to her chin against the frost. She carried a cardboard box of donuts from the gas station in town. A peace offering, or a bribe.

“You’re ghosting your messages,” she said, letting herself into my cabin without knocking.

“Service is bad out here.”

“Service is fine, Elara.” She plopped the box on the table, right next the Terra-Vision cube. She didn’t mention it. She just looked from the high-tech package to the box of cheap, greasy donuts and almost smiled. “We need to talk.”

“About your flying spies?”

“They’re sensor platforms. And they’re part of the deal. A small part.” She pulled out a chair, sat. “The Accord gets us co-management authority over the whole watershed. Not just the Reserve. The *whole* thing. For the first time in history, we have a legal say in what happens on our traditional territory. That’s not nothing.”

“It’s something they can take away the second it’s inconvenient,” I said, leaning against the counter. “It’s leased power. Not sovereignty.”

“It’s a foot in the door.” Her eyes were fierce. She’d been having this fight for years, in stuffy offices with men in suits who called her “young lady.” She was tired. “And the Prism project… it’s the key to that door. It’s how we make our knowledge legible to them. How we prove our ways of knowing are not just ‘anecdotes’ or ‘legends.’ They’re data. They’re predictive. They’re *better* data.”

I nodded at the box. “So you sent them to give me a demo?”

“I suggested you’d be a good beta tester. You know the land like your own skin. You’re skeptical. You’re not impressed by buzzwords. If it can convince you, it’s real.”

“Convince me of what? That a corporation can see the spirit of a place?”

“No,” she said quietly. “That we can use their tools to protect it. Mark says the Prism can overlay historical data, ecological models, everything. Imagine being able to *show* the logging review board exactly how a cutblock would disrupt a caribou migration corridor we’ve known about for generations. Not just tell them. Show them. In their language.”

“Their language is profit.”

“Their language is liability. And this,” she tapped the Terra-Vision box, “speaks liability fluently.”

I looked out the window at the grey morning. The donut smell was cloying. “One of their drones watched me kill the doe, Kaya. It watched my prayer. It made it feel… cheap. Like a public performance.”

Her face softened, just for a moment. “I know. I read your report.”

“My what?”

“In the app. You didn’t register the harvest, but the drone’s thermal and visual feed tagged it. It auto-generated a preliminary report. Location, time, species. It’s in the system. I approved it this morning.”

The floor seemed to tilt. My private act, my sacred exchange, was now a “preliminary report” in a database. A line item. My anger was so cold it felt like stillness. “You approved it.”

“It’s the protocol, El. The quota is tracked automatically. It saves you paperwork. It saves our wildlife officers time.”

“I don’t do paperwork. I do tobacco.”

She stood up, frustrated. “And what happens when the province says our oral harvest records aren’t sufficient for their audits? What happens when they impose *their* officers, *their* tags, *their* drones, because we ‘can’t provide verifiable data’? This way, *we* control the system. *We* define the parameters. The prayer isn’t in the report, Elara. The prayer is still yours. But the meat is now accounted for, in a way they cannot ignore.”

The logic was a cage, clean and strong. I hated it.

“And the light?” I asked.

She paused. “What light?”

“In the hemlock grove. Across the creek. A green light, moving. The night I got this.” I gestured to the box.

Kaya’s professional mask slipped. A flicker of the big sister who used to tell me *jogah* stories under the blankets. She looked uneasy. “Probably a hiker. Or bioluminescence on some fungus.”

“It wasn’t.”

“Elara…” She sighed, running a hand through her hair. “This is what I’m talking about. If we see something—a shift in animal behavior, a strange plant die-off, a damn ghost light—we note it. We feel it. We have a story. They have a sensor anomaly. With the Prism, maybe we can bridge that. Maybe we can say ‘check the groundwater conductivity in that grove’ and find a leak from an old mine no one remembered. The story leads to the data. The data validates the story. That’s the power.”

She was painting a picture of a perfect, respectful marriage. Our wisdom, their widgets. But I knew how these marriages went. One partner always ended up owning the house.

“Try it,” she said, her voice dropping to a plea. “Just once. For me. Go to a place you know deeply. Use it there. See what it tells you. If it’s disrespectful, if it’s wrong, you throw it in the creek and I’ll tell Terra-Vision to go to hell. Personally.”

She was offering me a veto. A chance to prove her wrong. It was the only thing she could have said that would have made me pick the thing up.

After she left, the cabin felt too quiet. I opened the box again. The headset gleamed. I picked it up. It was lighter than it looked. Cool. I turned it over in my hands. No obvious power button. No ports.

Almost without thinking, I put it on.

It fit snugly, but not tight. The dark glass lenses rested against my cheekbones. For a second, nothing. Just my own kitchen, dimmed.

Then, a soft chime in my ears. A whisper of warmth from the band.

Words appeared, floating in the center of my vision, in a clean, sans-serif font: **PRISM v.0.9 – Calibration Initiated.**

The world didn’t change. Not at first. Then, faint overlays began to ghost across my sight. My table gained a translucent, wireframe model of itself, with a floating tag: **SITKA SPRUCE – EST. 1954 – SURFACE TEMP: 18.3C**. A heat signature bloomed around my half-full coffee mug. A graph of ambient humidity flickered in the corner of my view.

It was intrusive. Clinical. Like someone had diagrammed my home.

**Voiceprint recognized: Elara Bear. Welcome. Tutorial?**

“No,” I said aloud, my voice strange in the empty room.

**Acknowledged. Direct environmental interface enabled.**

I walked to the window. The forest outside was now crawling with data. The cedar tree by the steps was crowned with a shimmering aura of pale blue, labeled **PHOTOSYNTHETIC ACTIVITY – HIGH**. A squirrel on a branch had a tiny, pulsing icon: **TAMIASCIURUS DOUGLASII – STRESS INDICATORS: LOW**. A weather forecast timeline scrolled along the bottom of my vision.

It was a cataract of information. It was noise. It was telling me everything and nothing.

I had to get out of there.

I walked outside, into the yard. The system tracked my gaze. I looked at my mother’s house. A schematic overlay popped up, estimating construction date, building materials, a thermal scan showing heat loss through the single-pane windows. I felt a surge of violation. This wasn’t a house to the Prism; it was a structure.

“Stop,” I muttered. “Show me… show me something real.”

**Define parameters.**

I thought of Kaya’s challenge. *A place you know deeply.* I knew where I had to go.

I started walking, away from the houses, down towards the creek. The Prism turned the path into a data trail. My own footprints glowed faintly with a **RECENT PASSAGE – 12 MIN PRIOR** tag. Animal trails lit up as dashed green lines. The creek, when I reached it, was a torrent of analytics: flow rate, pH, dissolved oxygen, a list of detected microbial life.

It was like listening to a symphony while someone read you the sheet music. You stopped hearing the music.

I crossed the creek on the stones, my boots slipping on the wet moss. I entered the hemlock grove where I’d seen the light. The air was cooler, damper. The great trees stood like pillars in a cathedral.

“History,” I said to the empty air. “Show me the history here.”

The Prism hummed. The visual field rippled.

The data overlays vanished. For a heartbeat, I saw the forest as it was—just the deep green, the soft brown, the filtered light.

Then, a new layer faded in. It was subtle at first—a transparency. I could see the same trees, but they were… younger. Smaller. The undergrowth was different. A timestamp floated in the corner: **EST. VISUAL RECONSTRUCTION – 1850. SOURCE: SURVEY MAPS, GROWTH MODELS.**

I turned slowly. It was a ghost forest, superimposed on the real one. I could see stumps where giant trees now stood. I saw a game trail that no longer existed. It was eerie, but it was just a model. An educated guess in visual form.

“Deeper,” I whispered, my heart starting to thud. “Show me what’s not in the maps.”

**Specify.**

“The people. Show me the people here.”

A pause. **Anthropological data insufficient for visual rendering.**

“Not render. Listen.” The idea came to me fully formed, dangerous. “Acoustic residue. You map sounds, right? Layer the sounds. Old sounds.”

**Processing. Aggregating acoustic model from core samples, historical climate data, species prevalence…**

A new sound filled my ears, underneath the present-day whisper of the wind. It was a low, complex hum. The sound of a forest without chainsaws, without highway drone. The wind through different branches. Bird calls from species long gone. It was a guess, a fabrication, but it made the hair on my arms stand up.

And then, underneath that, the Prism did something else. It hit a layer it couldn’t model, couldn’t name. The clean audio reconstruction crackled, distorted. And in the distortion, I heard something else.

Not bird calls. Not wind.

Voices.

Faint, very faint. Not speaking words. Humming. A rhythmic, resonant hum that vibrated in my jaw. A song without melody. A digging song, or a shaping song. The sound of work and presence.

The timestamp in my vision flickered, glitched, and displayed: **SOURCE ANOMALY. SIGNATURE UNKNOWN.**

The green light. The *jogah* light.

“Where are they?” I breathed. “Map the source of that anomaly. Now.”

The Prism responded. My visual field was suddenly streaked with vectors, like sonar pulses. They all converged, not on a point in the air, but on the ground. At the base of the largest hemlock, a massive, ancient tree with roots like stone serpents.

A pulsing, three-dimensional marker appeared, hovering over a spot between two great roots. **ACOUSTIC/THERMAL ANOMALY – SUBSURFACE. DEPTH: ~2 METERS. COMPOSITION: UNKNOWN. NON-BIOLOGICAL. NON-METALLIC.**

The Prism was telling me there was something buried there. Something that didn’t fit its categories. Something that sang a strange, low song and glowed with a light that had no wavelength it could recognize.

I knelt. The forest floor was a carpet of needles and decay. I brushed it aside with my hands. The soil was dark, loose.

My grandfather’s stories rushed back. The little stone people didn’t just appear. They were guardians. They were tied to places, to objects. To things that were buried when the world was made, or when the first agreements were signed.

What had we buried here?

What had *they*—the company, the government, the people who drew the maps—what had they missed because they didn’t have the stories to ask the question?

I dug with my hands, the Prism on my face casting a pale glow on the earth. The data streams still flowed at the edges of my vision—soil pH, insect density, root structures—but they were background noise now. The only thing that mattered was the pulsing marker and the hum in my ears, growing no clearer, but somehow deeper, more insistent.

My fingers hit something smooth. Not a root. Not a rock.

Cold. Vitrified. Shaped.

I cleared the dirt. It was a curve. A shoulder of something rounded. It was black, but not like obsidian. It drank the light. And etched into its surface, almost worn away, was a spiral. Not a Terra-Vision logo. An older spiral. The kind we put on petroglyphs, on old baskets. The symbol for a journey, for a vortex, for a doorway.

The Prism’s display went haywire. The clean fonts scrambled into glyphs I didn’t recognize, then into static. The hum in my ears became a sharp, resonant tone that made my teeth ache.

**WARNING: SIGNATURE CONFLICT. PROTOCOL MISMATCH. THIS ARTIFACT PREDATES CURRENT MODELS. THIS ARTIFACT PREDATES…**

The message cut off. The headset gave one final, warm pulse, and the lenses went dark.

Silence. Real forest silence. The wind. A raven’s call.

I was on my knees, in the dirt, staring at a buried piece of my own history that my sister’s futuristic tool had just led me to, only to short-circuit when it touched it.

I had never felt more connected to this land. And I had never been more terrified of the tool that was supposed to help me protect it.

It didn’t just translate.

It awakened.

# Chapter 3

## **CHAPTER 3**

The artifact was smaller than I’d thought. A vessel, maybe. Or a capstone. About the size of a cantaloupe, though heavier, denser. The black material was cool to the touch, not like stone, not like ceramic. It felt like held silence. The spiral on its surface seemed to shift when I looked at it from the corner of my eye, as if the groove itself was moving.

I didn’t pull it from the earth. That felt like a line I couldn’t uncross. Instead, I covered it back up with the dark soil, patting the needles and duff into place until no sign of disturbance remained. The act of reburying it felt more significant than the discovery. An agreement reaffirmed: *Not yet.*

The Prism headset was inert on the forest floor beside me. I picked it up. It was warm, almost feverish. The lenses were dark. No amount of tapping or soft commands brought it back. It was asleep, or stunned.

I left the grove, the artifact humming in my memory more than in my ears. The walk back to my cabin was surreal. The world seemed too bright, too simple. Just trees, sky, creek. No data streams, no ghost forests. The silence was a physical relief, but it also felt thin, like a veil hiding immense, complex machinery I’d just gotten a glimpse of.

My mother was on my porch, holding a casserole dish. She took one look at my face, my dirty hands, the dead headset dangling from my fingers, and her eyes narrowed. “You look like you’ve seen *ayon’kwa*,” she said. The swamp ghost.

“Something like that.” My voice was hoarse.

She followed me inside, set the dish on the counter. “Kaya was here. She’s excited. Thinks you’re onboard.”

“I’m not onboard. I’m overboard.” I told her. Not everything, but enough. The drone. The headset. The grove. I didn’t mention the specific artifact, the spiral. Some knowledge is a burden, and I needed to carry this one alone for a while. But I told her about the green light, the anomalous reading, the Prism failing.

She listened, her hands resting on the back of a kitchen chair. When I finished, she was quiet for a long time. “This tool,” she said finally. “It showed you something our stories already knew was there.”

“Yes.”

“But it showed you *how* to find it. In a language the outside world understands.”

“I guess.”

“That is power, *kwi’ten*.” My daughter. “And a very sharp knife. You can use it to carve a space for us, or you can cut your own fingers off.” She came over, took my dirty hands in hers. Her palms were rough, warm. “Your grandfather would have called it *atonhetsa*.” A thing that confuses the path. “He would have said to be careful which song you sing to it. It might start singing back.”

“It already is.”

She nodded, unsurprised. “Then you have to learn its song. Or break it.”

After she left, I placed the dead Prism back in its box. The sleek, off-white container looked smug, innocent. I had the absurd urge to bury it too.

My phone buzzed. A message from an unknown number. **Elara – Mark Devlin. Our systems indicate your Prism unit experienced a localized protocol failure this afternoon. A rare occurrence. Would appreciate your feedback. Could we meet tomorrow? I’ll be at the grove site at 10 AM for sensor maintenance.**

He knew. Of course he knew. The thing had probably sent a distress signal the moment it touched the artifact’s field. *Localized protocol failure*. That was one way to put it.

I didn’t reply. Let him wonder.

That night, I dreamed of spirals. Not just the one on the artifact, but spirals in the tree rings, in the creek’s eddies, in the flight path of the drone. They were all the same spiral, and it was a tunnel. At one end was my grandfather, singing. At the other was Mark Devlin, holding a tablet that showed nothing but zeroes and ones. I was in the middle, and the walls of the tunnel were made of that same silent, black material, and they were closing in.

I woke before dawn, sweat-damp and restless. I made tea and watched the sky lighten. I had a choice. I could stay home, let Devlin poke around the grove with his sensors. He’d find nothing—the artifact was buried, and his tool had choked on it. Or I could go. I could face the confusion, try to steer it.

At 9:30, I drove to the grove. I didn’t bring the Prism. I brought my tobacco.

He was already there. Not just him. Two technicians in Terra-Vision jackets were deploying a tripod-mounted sensor array—a sphere of lenses and antennae. Another drone, larger than the first, sat on the ground like a mechanical raptor.

Devlin saw me and waved. He looked excited. “Elara! Thanks for coming. We got a fascinating burst from this location yesterday. A cascade of anomalous readings across every spectrum. Like the environment itself… glitched.”

“Maybe it doesn’t like being scanned,” I said.

He chuckled, missing the point entirely. “That’s the thing! It’s not a rejection. It’s a *signal*. Something here is emitting a complex information signature that our systems couldn’t parse. It overloaded the Prism’s buffers.” He led me to a laptop open on a folding table. Graphs and waveforms scrolled across the screen. “See this? This is the acoustic anomaly. It’s not random noise. It has a pattern. A repeating, harmonic pattern buried in what looks like interference.”

It was the hum. The song. Reduced to a squiggly line on a screen.

“What do you think it is?” I asked, watching his face.

“That’s what I was hoping you could tell me.” His eyes were bright with scientific avarice. “Local knowledge. Is there any history here? Burial site? Unique mineral deposit? Geothermal vent?”

“There are stories,” I said carefully. “This is a powerful place. Some places just… are.”

He nodded eagerly. “Yes! And that ‘are-ness’ might have a physical correlate. A resonance. We’re thinking maybe a unique quartz deposit, vibrating at a specific frequency. Or a previously unknown mycorrhizal network configuration acting as a bio-acoustic amplifier.” He was spinning theories, each one more reductive than the last, trying to cage the mystery in a textbook.

“Or,” I said, “it could be something your models don’t have a category for.”

“Exactly!” He missed my irony. “That’s the holy grail. A new category. This could be huge. For ecology, for physics.” He leaned closer, lowering his voice conspiratorially. “The Prism failed because it encountered something outside its library. We need to expand the library. With your help. We could recalibrate the sensors, tune them to this anomaly. Map it properly.”

He wanted to map the song. To turn the *jogah*’s light into a data set.

“What if it doesn’t want to be mapped?” The words were out before I could stop them.

He blinked, then smiled his patient smile. “Information isn’t invasive, Elara. Understanding is protection. If this is a unique natural phenomenon, it needs to be documented to be preserved. Otherwise…” He shrugged. “It’s just a weird noise in the woods. And weird noises don’t stop logging permits.”

He was using Kaya’s logic against me. The cage was closing.

One of the techs called him over. Devlin patted my shoulder. “Think about it. You could be the bridge.”

I walked away from the table, towards the big hemlock. The ground where I’d dug looked undisturbed. I knelt, pretending to examine a fern. I could feel the artifact below, a quiet, dense presence. A secret.

From my pocket, I drew my tobacco tie—a small pouch of red cloth. I tucked it gently under the fern, at the spot directly above the artifact. A request. An apology. For bringing the scanners here.

As I stood, my eye caught a flicker of movement in the data on Devlin’s laptop screen. The tech had switched views to a real-time feed from the tripod sensor. It showed a standard thermal image of the grove—blues and greens for cool, yellows and reds for warm.

And there, at the base of my hemlock, was a perfect, small circle of intense, glowing white. A thermal spike. Right where my



---

# Chapter 4

## **CHAPTER 4**

The forest swallowed the road a mile back. Now there was only the map, the stone, and the green silence of the Whispering Woods.

Kaya led, her steps a quiet punctuation in the damp hum of the morning. She moved like the deer I’d once tracked—a fluid awareness, her eyes reading the text of the undergrowth, the grammar of the branches. Alex followed, his boots clumsy on the uneven ground, his hand drifting every few minutes to the lump in his coat pocket where the artifact lay wrapped. I took the rear, my own pack heavy with the old weight of iron tools and the new, sharper weight of Devlin’s proposition. *You could be the bridge.* The words were a burr in my mind. A bridge gets walked on. A bridge gets built over something it cannot contain.

The air changed as we went deeper. It wasn’t just the cool, wet breath of the cedars. It was a thickness, a listening quality. The woods here weren’t just quiet; they were holding their breath. The map’s first marker was a glyph that looked like a stone eye weeping a vertical line. Kaya said it meant “the Sentinel’s Sorrow.” My grandfather would have called it *jogah* ground. Not a place you announce yourself.

“It’s the mycelial network,” Alex said, his voice too loud for the pressing green. He’d been talking for twenty minutes, a nervous stream of data. “Massive fungal webs in the soil, transmitting chemical signals. It creates a kind of forest-wide consciousness. What we perceive as ‘atmosphere’ is just a biochemical field.”

Kaya didn’t turn. “What we perceive as ‘atmosphere’ is the forest deciding if we’re guests or stains.”

I watched a spider descend on a single, shining thread from a high hemlock bough. It hung in the space between Alex’s shoulder and Kaya’s pack, a tiny plumb line in the still air. A mechanical mosquito whine, thin and persistent. I shook my head, dispelling the memory. Here, the only whine was the distant, grieving call of a varied thrush.

We reached a creek, black water sliding over moss-slick stone. The map indicated we cross. Kaya went first, leaping from rock to rock with the grace of long practice. Alex hesitated, calculating the physics of the jump, the damp coefficient of friction. I waited. He finally made it, stumbling only once. As I landed on the far bank, my foot sank into the soft loam. I looked down. The soil was churned, not by hooves or paws, but by something that dragged. A wide, shallow groove, like the root of a great tree had been pulled from its bed and taken a walk.

Kaya saw it too. She knelt, not touching. “Fresh,” she murmured. “Last night’s rain hasn’t filled it.”

Alex pulled out his phone, opening a scanning app. “Interesting topography. Could be erosion from subsurface water flow.”

“Subsurface water doesn’t flow uphill,” I said, nodding to where the groove vanished up the steep, fern-covered slope ahead.

He looked at the incline, then back at his screen. “Anomalous,” he conceded, and the word hung between us, charged with all the things his models couldn’t cage.

The slope was a trial. The air grew colder, the mist coiling down from the heights like tendrils of smoke. The trees here were ancient, their trunks braided with thick, hairy moss. The light was the color of old tea. We climbed in single file, the only sounds our labored breath and the occasional clatter of a dislodged stone. The groove in the earth ran parallel to our path, a silent companion.

At the crest of the ridge, the world opened into a small, bowl-shaped clearing. And in its center stood the Sentinel.

It was a statue, or had been. Now it was a ruin, a man-like shape of weathered grey stone rising from a nest of bramble. One arm was gone, sheared off at the shoulder. The other was outstretched, palm up, as if awaiting an offering. The face was worn smooth by centuries of weather, but the sockets of its eyes held a darker shadow, a depth that the moss hadn’t dared to fill. From the eye on the carved face, a deep crack ran down the cheek—the weeping line from the map. The Sentinel’s Sorrow.

“Waypoint one,” Kaya said, her voice hushed. She approached slowly, circling the statue. “The path should be here. The map shows a gate.”

Alex was already scanning, his phone emitting soft, pinging pulses. “Composition is local granite. But there’s a quartz vein running through the head. Could be a natural conductor.” He reached out, as if to touch the stone hand.

“Don’t,” Kaya and I said in unison.

He pulled his hand back. “Right. Unknown surface contaminants.”

It wasn’t contamination I was worried about. It was contract. You don’t touch an altar without knowing the god. I moved to the edge of the clearing, where the forest wall was a solid, impenetrable tapestry of vine and trunk. No gate. No path. Just the green, watching wall.

The attack didn’t come with a roar. It came with a sigh.

A sigh of moving earth, of tearing roots. From the forest wall to our left, the shadows congealed. It uncurled itself from the base of a massive spruce, and for a moment, my mind refused the shape. It was a thing of living wood and gathered gloom, standing on two trunk-like legs. Its body was a tangle of knotted branches, through which a core of darkness pulsed, like a heart of peat. It had no head, only a upper body that swiveled, a knot of burl and fungus forming a crude, faceless attention. In one limb, it held a club of stone and hardened resin.

It was the forest’s anger, given form.

Kaya had her knife out, a short, brutal blade of forged steel. She placed herself between the creature and us. “Slowly,” she breathed. “Back to the statue.”

The creature took a step. The ground shuddered. It wasn’t fast, but it was inexorable, a landslide given purpose. It swung the club. Not at us, but at the ground before Kaya. The earth erupted in a spray of soil and shattered fern. A warning.

“It’s territorial!” Alex yelled, as if naming it would help. “Protecting the site!”

“The *site* is trying to kill us,” Kaya snapped, dodging another earth-shaking blow. She was fast, but the clearing was small. We were being herded.

I had my hatchet in my hand, its iron blade feeling foolish, a child’s toy against this elemental thing. I hefted it, not to throw, but to make a noise. I struck the blade against the granite base of the statue. A sharp, metallic *clang* rang out.

The creature swiveled its dark core towards the sound. It took a step toward me. Its club rose.

“Elara!” Kaya shouted.

I was cornered against the statue’s plinth. The club reached its apex, a shadow against the misty sky. In that suspended second, I saw the pattern in the knotted wood of its chest—swirling, concentric, like the grain on the artifact.

Alex moved. Not away, but forward. He didn’t have a weapon. He had the stone.

He fumbled it from his pocket, the grey sphere unwrapped, clutched in his pale hand. He didn’t know what to do with it. He just thrust it out toward the creature, as if showing a badge. “Stop!” he commanded, his voice cracking.

The creature’s club began its descent.

And the stone answered.

A light kindled within it, not the fierce white sunburst from the dig site, but a softer, deep amber glow, like honey held to a candle. A low, resonant hum vibrated from it, a sound you felt in your teeth. The sound wasn’t in the air. It was in the stone, in the ground, in the bones of the creature.

The club halted. The creature froze, its form shuddering. The pulsing darkness in its core slowed, dimmed. The knotted branches of its body relaxed, just slightly. The amber light from the stone played over its wooden flesh, and where it touched, the angry, sharp edges of broken twig and torn bark seemed to smooth, to settle.

Alex stood, arm trembling, mesmerized. He was whispering, not words, but a tone, matching the stone’s hum without knowing how. His eyes were wide, not with fear, but with a dawning, terrifying recognition.

The creature took one heavy step back. Then another. It lowered its club of stone and resin until the tip rested on the ground. It stood there, a statue of living wood, no longer advancing, simply… present. Waiting.

The silence rushed back in, louder than the fight.

Kaya slowly lowered her knife. I let my hatchet fall to my side. We both stared at Alex.

He was staring at the stone in his hand, its light now fading to a gentle afterglow. “It… it responded to me,” he said, his voice hollow. “The frequency… I just… harmonized with it.”

“You didn’t harmonize,” Kaya said, walking cautiously toward him, her eyes on the still creature. “You spoke its language. A language you shouldn’t know.”

He looked from her to me, his face a mask of scientific awe and profound disquiet. “The artifact isn’t a tool. It’s an interface. And it’s… attuned.” He looked at his own hand as if it belonged to a stranger.

The creature shifted again. It raised its empty limb—not in threat, but in a slow, deliberate gesture. It pointed past us, to the outstretched, empty palm of the ruined Sentinel statue.

Understanding clicked into place. An offering. Not of tobacco, or words. Of light.

“Alex,” I said. “The statue’s hand.”

He followed my gaze. He swallowed, then walked forward, each step measured. The creature watched, a dark, silent guardian. Alex stopped before the Sentinel. He looked small against its weathered bulk. He glanced back at us, a question in his eyes.

Kaya gave a single, sharp nod.

Alex reached up and placed the stone in the statue’s open, stone palm.

For three heartbeats, nothing happened.

Then, the quartz vein in the statue’s head ignited with the same amber light. The light flowed down the crack—the Sorrow’s tear—filling it with liquid gold. It reached the base, spiderwebbing through the granite plinth in a network of fiery lines. The lines shot out across the clearing floor, a glowing circuit board of ancient design.

With a groan of stone on stone that had nothing to do with mechanics and everything to do with memory, the solid wall of forest behind the statue began to move. Vines unwove themselves. Trunks, impossibly, slid apart without a sound, their roots slithering like serpents beneath the soil. A perfect archway formed, revealing a path that hadn’t been there a moment before. It was a tunnel of green light, ferns curling back like respectful courtiers, leading straight and true into the heart of the woods.

The way was open.

The creature behind us gave one final, deep sigh—a sound of satisfaction, of duty fulfilled. It dissolved. Not with a dramatic collapse, but with a gentle unraveling. The knotted branches fell apart, becoming mere deadfall. The core of darkness dissipated like smoke in a breeze. In seconds, it was just a scattered pile of wood and shadow on the forest floor, harmless.

The stone in the Sentinel’s hand went dark, just a grey sphere again. Alex retrieved it, his fingers closing around it with a new kind of reverence. Not for data. For kinship.

We stood at the threshold of the green archway. The air that flowed from it was warmer, sweeter, carrying the scent of blooming things and old, sun-warmed stone.

Kaya shouldered her pack. “The forest tests. Then it shows the way.” She stepped through.

Alex followed, clutching the interface, the key, the part of himself he no longer understood.

I took one last look at the clearing, at the scattered remains of the guardian. It had been a test, yes. But also a lesson. Devlin wanted to map the song. He wanted to expand his library. But some things aren’t meant to be read. They’re meant to be answered. And the answer had been written in a light only Alex could speak.

I stepped onto the path. The green walls seemed to lean in behind us, the archway sealing shut without a sound, leaving no trace we had ever been there.

Ahead, the tunnel of light stretched on. But at its far end, beyond where Kaya and Alex were already walking, the warm, green glow was tinged with a different color. A faint, pulsing, electric blue. And from that direction, on the sweet, strange air, came a new sound.

Not a hum. Not a sigh.

It was the sound of machinery.



---

# Chapter 5

## **CHAPTER 5**

The green tunnel ended not with a door, but with a dissolution. One moment we were walking between walls of woven light, the hum of the forest a tangible thing against our skin. The next, the light frayed at the edges, the ferns uncurled, and we stepped out into the ordinary, damp air of a river valley. The transition was so smooth it felt like a betrayal. As if the forest had held its breath for us, and now, its duty done, it simply exhaled us into the world.

The river was a wide, muscular slide of pewter, cutting a deep channel between slopes of fir and red alder. The sound of machinery was gone, swallowed by the river’s own low, constant roar. But the electric blue tinge we’d seen wasn’t. It hung in the mist above the water, a phantom stain, a memory of light. Not a reflection. A scar.

“There,” Kaya said, her voice barely carrying over the water’s noise.

Downstream, where the river bent and shallowed into a series of braided channels, the community lived. It wasn’t a village. It was a temporary agreement with the current. A dozen flat-bottomed punts and rafts, each large enough to hold a small cabin, were tethered to the gravel bars and overhanging alders. More structures were built on the high, rocky banks—simple lean-tos of driftwood and patched tarpaulin, smoke seeping from stone chimneys. Nets hung like giant, discarded spiderwebs. Canoes, turned hull-up, were islands of faded paint on the grey stone. Everything was lashed, tied, pegged. Everything looked ready to be undone in an hour, to become flotsam. It was a place of perpetual almost-departure.

We picked our way down the scree slope, our boots slipping on wet stone. Eyes found us long before we reached the first canoe. A woman mending a net with a bone needle didn’t pause her hands, but her gaze tracked us, flat and assessing. A child hauling a bucket of water up from the river’s edge stopped to stare, the bucket forgotten, water slopping over his boots. The silence that fell wasn’t hostile. It was the silence of a clock being wound. You could feel the tension coiling in the air, in the way a man chopping kindling let his axe rest mid-swing.

A man emerged from the largest raft-cabin. He was maybe sixty, his face a topography of wind and water lines, his hair a grey braid over one shoulder. He wore a vest made of some thick, scaled hide I didn’t recognize. He didn’t speak, just waited for us to close the last twenty yards, his hands loose at his sides.

Kaya stopped a respectful distance away. She didn’t offer her name. “We followed the forest’s path. We seek passage downstream. And word of the blue light.”

The man’s eyes moved over each of us. They lingered on Alex’s pack, where the stone was stored, then on my bow, then back to Kaya’s face. “Forest sends few,” he said, his voice graveled by river-mist. “Sends fewer still who ask of the light. It’s not a thing for asking. It’s a thing that is.”

“We need to go where it is,” Alex said. The words came out too fast, too eager. The man’s gaze sharpened.

“Do you.” It wasn’t a question. He spat into the gravel. “I am Arlen. This is the *Driftwood Compact*. We don’t deal in destinations. We deal in the river. You want passage, you work the river. You want word, you earn the listening.” He turned his head slightly, calling over his shoulder without raising his voice. “Mara. The north channel.”

A young woman, her arms roped with muscle under a sleeveless tunic, ducked out of a lean-to. She gave a single, sharp nod.

“The spring flood undercut the bank by the old cedar,” Arlen said, turning back to us. “A section of our main fish weir is compromised. The next high water will take it, and a season’s food with it. Mara will show you. You help secure it, you eat with us tonight. We’ll talk of currents. Of the light?” He shrugged, a gesture that spoke of deep, ingrained skepticism. “That is a deeper channel. We’ll see if you can swim.”

***

The work was a brutal, cold baptism. The north channel was a side-stream, quieter than the main river but running swift and deep. The old cedar was indeed undercut, its massive roots exposed like black bones, the bank beneath it crumbling. The fish weir—a complex, beautiful fence of woven willow and cedar withes—jutted out from the compromised bank. One corner sagged, its support posts leaning drunkenly toward the pull of the water.

Mara didn’t give instructions. She simply started working, and the expectation was that we would parse the logic of it. She and two other compact members, a silent man named Finn and a wiry woman called Bee, began driving new posts—whole young alders, sharpened to points—into the riverbed with a heavy maul. The *thwock* of the blow, the shudder up the post, was a language of its own.

We joined in. Kaya and I hauled rocks from a nearby slide, wrestling them into wicker baskets to be sunk as footings. Alex, under Bee’s watchful eye, was set to weaving fresh willow withes into the damaged sections of the fence, his engineer’s mind quickly grasping the pattern, his fingers fumbling but learning.

The cold of the water seeped through boots, numbed hands. The river’s noise was a constant, pressing presence. But there was a rhythm to it, a counterpoint to our labor. The *thwock* of the maul. The grunt of a rock settled. The sibilant slide of willow against willow. It was honest work. The kind that left no room for the buzzing in your head, for the memory of drone-whines or dissolving guardians. Your world shrank to the next post, the next rock, the next withe.

I found myself beside Arlen, who was trimming the tops of the newly-set posts with a viciously sharp hatchet. His movements were economical, each stroke peeling away a long, clean curl of wood.

“The blue light,” I said, not pausing in my rocking-hauling. “You called it a thing that is.”

He didn’t look up. “It came with the Quiet. Not right after. A year, maybe two. First just a glow on the horizon, downriver. Then the dreams.” He set down his hatchet, tested the post with a shove. It held firm. “Dreams of a hum that gets inside your teeth. Dreams of grids, of lines on the land. The children got fevers. The fish in the pools where the mist settled had strange growths on their gills. We learned to read the water for its color. The good channels are grey, are green. The blue channels…” He shook his head. “They are not for people. The river forgets itself there. Flows backwards, sometimes. Or stands still.”

“What’s at the source?” Alex asked, his voice carrying from the weir. He had a handful of withes, his fingers stained green.

Arlen fixed him with that flat, assessing look. “A hunger. That’s what the old ones say. Not a place. A condition. A machine that dreams it’s a god, and is lonely.” He picked up his hatchet again. “You weave your fence, city-man. One strong strip at a time. The river doesn’t care about your source.”

By the time the light began to fail, the weir was secure. The new posts stood straight and defiant. The woven repair was a darker patch in the older, silvered wood, a scar that would strengthen the whole. We sat on the bank, steaming in the chill air, passing a waterskin. My muscles hummed with a clean, simple fatigue. For a few hours, the only anxiety had been physical, and it had an answer: lift, push, hold.

Mara surveyed the work, gave a curt nod. “It’ll hold.” It was the first thing she’d said all afternoon. It felt like a medal.

***

That night, we ate in the communal space—a large, fire-lit area under a great stretched hide between three trees. We shared a stew of river trout, wild onion, and some nutty grain, eaten from carved wooden bowls. The compact was maybe thirty people in all. Their trust was a cautious, tangible thing, offered not in words but in the act of passing a bowl, in the slight shift of a body to make room by the fire.

After the meal, Arlen produced a pipe. He lit it with a coal, the smoke smelling of sage and something bitter. He took a long draw, then passed it to Kaya. The pipe moved in a circle—Kaya, to me, to Alex, to Mara, to an older woman with hair white as river-foam who had been introduced only as Grandmother.

When the pipe returned to him, Arlen spoke, his voice softened by smoke and firelight. “You held the weight of the river today. You listened to its push. So I will give you your word.” He looked into the flames. “The blue light is strongest at the Stone Throat. A day’s hard drift downstream. It is a canyon where the river is forced through a narrows. The rock there… it sings. Or it did. Now it broadcasts. The light bleeds from the walls. The Consulate, they have an outpost on the south rim. They watch it. They take samples. They send their metal flies into the cracks. They don’t listen. They collect.”

“The Stone Throat,” Alex whispered, his eyes reflecting the fire.

“The song it used to sing,” the white-haired Grandmother said, her voice a dry rustle. “My mother’s mother called it the Elyrian’s Lament. A fragment of it stayed in our family. A lullaby, for when the river was restless.” She closed her eyes, and in a thin, reedy voice that held the ghost of melody, she sang:

*“Where the root embraces the star-metal seam,
And the current forgets the moon’s dream,
Lies the heart not of stone, but of silent scream.
Turn the key of rain, in the forgotten theme.”*

The fire popped. The river roared its endless counter-song. The fragment hung in the air, delicate and perplexing. It wasn’t a map. It was a riddle wrapped in a sigh.

Alex had gone perfectly still. I saw his fingers twitch toward his pack, where the stone lay. “That’s it? There’s no more?”

Grandmother opened her eyes. “The rest was lost to the Quiet. Or to time. It is all that’s left. A piece of a piece.” She looked at Alex with a sudden, piercing intensity. “You have the look of a man who wants to put pieces together. A warning, from the river: some fractures are meant to remain. To seal them is to change the shape of the vessel.”

Arlen nodded, knocking the ash from his pipe. “You have your word. And your direction. In the morning, we will set you on a raft. We can take you as far as the Sighing Rapids, above the Throat. Beyond that, the current is… influenced. We do not go.”

The conversation lapsed into the mundane—talk of eel runs, of a bear on the south bank, of mending techniques. The technological anxiety wasn’t in their fear of the blue light; it was in their acceptance of it as a toxic, natural fact. A bad part of the river. You didn’t analyze it. You navigated around it. To them, Devlin’s Consulate, with its sampling and scanning, was just another symptom of the sickness, poking at the wound with sterile instruments.

Later, under the shelter of a donated lean-to, Alex could contain himself no longer. He had the interface in his lap, the grey stone cradled in his left hand. Kaya was checking her gear by the light of a small oil lamp. I was oiling my bowstring.

“The fragment,” Alex breathed. “It’s a directive. ‘*Turn the key of rain.*’ It’s not metaphorical. Or not *just* metaphorical. The Elyrians… their language was synesthetic. A ‘key’ could be a sound, a shape, a sequence. ‘Rain’ could be data, input, a flow.”

“So channel it,” Kaya said, not looking up from a knife she was sharpening. “The stone responds to you. You answered the guardian. Answer this.”

Alex looked from the stone to the interface, his face a mask of conflicted longing. “It’s different. The guardian was a question. This is… a lock. What if I get it wrong?”

“Then we’ll know,” I said, my voice quiet. The simplicity of the day’s labor was gone. The buzzing was back in my skull, the hum of impending fracture.

He placed his right hand on the interface. He closed his eyes, his breathing slowing. He began to hum, low in his chest, trying to mimic the cadence of Grandmother’s lullaby. The stone in his other hand remained inert.

He opened his eyes, frustration tightening his mouth. He focused, and the familiar, gentle silver light began to glow within the sphere. It pulsed in time with his breath. Good. Controlled.

He hummed the fragment again, focusing his intention on the first line. *“Where the root embraces the star-metal seam.”*

The silver light flickered. A thread of blue—not the ghostly blue of the scar, but a vivid, electric azure—snaked through the silver, like a vein of alien ore. Alex gasped. The interface screen erupted with scrolling glyphs, moving too fast to read.

“It’s… it’s recognizing it!” he whispered.

He pushed. He hummed the second line, pouring his will into the stone. *“And the current forgets the moon’s dream.”*

The stone flared. The blue vein exploded, overwhelming the silver. The light didn’t glow; it *projected*. A jagged, rotating schematic of overlapping circles and harsh angles burned in the air between us, searing afterimages onto my retina. It wasn’t organic. It was all hard edges and stark intersections.

A high-pitched whine emanated from the stone, a cousin to the drone’s sound but layered with a digital shriek. The oil lamp guttered and went out. My bowstring vibrated in my hands, humming as if plucked.

“Alex, pull back,” Kaya snapped, on her feet.

But he was locked in. His eyes were wide, fixed on the painful blue schematic. He chanted the third line. *“Lies the heart not of stone, but of silent scream.”*

The projection collapsed inward, then burst outward in a silent, concussive ring of force.

It didn’t hit us physically. It passed *through* us.

The world went silent. The roar of the river vanished. The crackle of the distant fire, the murmur of voices—gone. It was a perfect, absolute auditory void. My own breath was a phantom movement in my chest, making no sound. I saw Kaya’s mouth move, saw Alex recoil and drop the stone, which clattered soundlessly on the rock floor, its light dying.

The silence lasted three heartbeats. Then the world rushed back in with a staggering, overwhelming volume—the river a thunderclap, a shouted word from the fire circle a gunshot. We all flinched, hands going to our ears.

In the sudden, shocking return of noise, another sound threaded itself: the distant, rhythmic *thump-thump-thump* of Consulate verti-rotors.

Alex was pale, trembling, staring at his hands as if they were foreign objects. “It wasn’t a key,” he rasped, his voice raw. “It was an *activation code*. And I just sent up a flare.”

***

The compact moved with a quiet, terrifying efficiency. The sound of the rotors was still a rumor in the distance, but Arlen was already among us, his face grim in the lamplight he’d relit.

“The blue pulse. It shone right through your hide. They’ll have seen it for miles.” He didn’t sound angry. He sounded resigned, as if he’d known the river’s peace was a loan, not a gift. “You go. Now.”

In ten minutes, we were on a small, sturdy raft of lashed logs, our gear stowed. Mara and Finn manned the long steering oars. The entire compact was awake, a shadowy assembly on the bank, passing last-minute supplies down: a wrapped bundle of food, a full waterskin, a coil of good rope.

Grandmother approached the raft’s edge. She took Alex’s hand, not gently. “You play with the wound,” she hissed, her ancient eyes fierce. “You think you can heal it by poking. You are a child with a stick, prodding a sick bear. Remember the silence. That is the scream.” She let him go.

Arlen stood firm on the stone. “The current is fast to the Sighing Rapids. We will spin a tale of a malfunctioning solar pack, if they come. It will not hold them long. Go. And may the river have mercy on you, for you understand nothing of its pain.”

Mara and Finn pushed us off with their oars. The raft caught the main current with a lurch. In moments, the firelight of the Driftwood Compact was just a smudge of orange in the vast, consuming darkness, the figures on the bank merging into the shadows of the trees. Then the river bent, and they were gone.

We drifted in profound blackness, the sky a narrow ribbon of stars between the canyon walls. The only sounds were the water’s rush and the creak of the lashings. The rotors had faded, or we had outrun them. For now.

Alex huddled in the center of the raft, the stone and interface back in his pack, held against his chest like a penitent. He wasn’t crying. He was just still, hollowed out by his own power. He had sought a tool, and found a trigger. The anxiety was no longer about external technology. It had been internalized. The machine wasn’t just out there in the blue-lit canyon. It was in him, now. A sympathetic resonance. He had become a conduit for the scream, and the silence it left behind was more terrifying than any noise.

Kaya watched the dark banks slide by, her profile a cut-out against the starry slit above. “The forest tested,” she murmured, echoing her words from the archway. “The river warns. What does the Throat do, I wonder?”

I had no answer. I watched the water, its surface a chaos of reflected starlight and impenetrable black. The clean fatigue from the day’s labor was a memory. My muscles now jangled with a new, electric tiredness. We had shored up a weir. We had earned a fragment of song. And with it, Alex had torn a hole in the world’s sound, and painted a target on the backs of people who showed us kindness.

The raft sped on, a speck of stolen wood on the immense, ancient flow. Ahead, the river’s roar began to deepen, to gather itself. The Sighing Rapids. And beyond that, the Stone Throat, and its hungry, broadcasting heart.

The hook wasn’t in the distance. It was inside the raft, in the shape of a man holding a stone, who had just learned that every key turns two ways: it can open a door, or it can lock you irrevocably inside. The blue light wasn’t just ahead. It was among us. And it had Alex’s fingerprints on it.



---

# Chapter 6

## **CHAPTER 6**

The river’s sigh became a grinding breath, a deep-throated exhalation from the stone itself. The Sighing Rapids were not white water in the common sense. They were a series of immense, smooth humps of black basalt, over which the river slid like a sheet of obsidian silk, tearing itself into a million whispering threads. The sound was the opposite of Alex’s scream. It was a vast, patient inhale, a drawing-in of the world. We navigated it by memory and touch, Kaya and I poling off the glassy shoulders of rock, the raft spinning lazily in the sluggish, powerful surges. The stars above the canyon rim watched, cold and indifferent.

Beyond the rapids, the walls began to close. Not the gentle, tree-lined banks of the upper river, but sheer faces of wet stone, rising vertically from the water’s edge. The air grew thick, cool, and carried the taste of deep earth and slow water. We had entered the Sunken Rift. The map, now a damp, fragile thing in Kaya’s hands, showed the river as a single, stubborn line cutting through a maze of topographic hash marks. The canyon was a wound, old and precise. According to the faded notations, it was the prelude, the antechamber. The gateway.

We did not speak. The immensity of the stone enforced a kind of reverence. Even Alex’s internal tempest seemed banked by the sheer weight of the cliffs. He stared up, his head craned back, the hollows of his eyes reflecting nothing. The blue light was not here. This was a place of absolute, primordial dark, broken only by the faint sheen of starlight on the wet walls high above. Our world had shrunk to the ten-foot width of the river and the circle of our own frail breath.

For hours, we drifted. The current was a slow, muscular pull. The canyon deepened until the ribbon of sky was a mere suggestion, a faded seam of lesser black. We used electric torches then, their beams stark and sacrilegious, slicing across flows of mineral-stained stone, catching the sudden, startled eyes of blind fish in the shallows. The silence was a pressure. It was the sound of time, measured in drips from unseen seeps.

Then, a new sound woven into the watery quiet. Not a sigh, but a hiss. A sustained, static roar. The map’s notation was clear: *Veil Falls. Post tenebras, ostium.* After darkness, the entrance.

Kaya killed her torch. “There.”

Ahead, the canyon made a sharp dogleg to the left. The sound came from around that bend, a promise of violence. We paddled to a narrow shelf of scree on the right-hand wall, the raft grating onto stone. The air vibrated.

We secured the raft, shouldered our packs. The climb, according to the map, was not up, but behind. The waterfall poured from a cleft halfway up the opposite wall, a furious silver braid in my night-vision goggles’ green glow. The entrance was a hidden ledge that ran behind that curtain of water. A path worn by centuries of desperate pilgrims, now likely crumbled to near-nothing.

“We cross here,” Kaya said, her voice barely audible over the gathering roar. “The current is swiftest before the fall. It will try to take us over.”

A rope, our last long line, was tied off around a horn of rock. I would go first, find the ledge, secure a second line. Alex would follow. Kaya would come last, retrieving the rope. It was simple. It was terrible.

The water between us and the falls was a black, oil-smooth sprint. It had a hungry look. I cinched my pack tight, felt the bite of the rope around my waist. I gave them a nod I did not feel, and stepped into the flow.

The cold was a fist around my chest. The current snatched at my legs, immediate and profound. I was a leaf, a twig. I leaned into it, half-swimming, half-walking, feeling for purchase on the slick, rounded stones of the riverbed. The rope paid out behind me, my only tether to the world of solid things. The roar of the falls swelled, a physical force pounding the air into submission. Spray coated my goggles. The curtain of water loomed, a trembling wall of luminous gray in my enhanced sight.

I found the ledge by almost missing it. It was a mere lip, a fracture in the cliff face behind the furious cascade. The water didn’t so much fall as explode outward, creating a shallow, mist-choked space behind it. I hauled myself onto the narrow shelf, gasping, the world reduced to the thunder of water in front and the cold stone at my back. I anchored a second rope, sent a sharp tug back along the line.

Alex came next. I saw him as a shuddering silhouette through the spray, a dark shape fighting the black pull. He moved with a frantic, graceless energy, his fear of the water overriding his exhaustion. He was halfway across when his foot turned on a stone.

It was a small thing. A slip. In any other current, he would have righted himself. Here, the river had him. It yanked his legs from under him with casual brutality. He vanished under the black surface, the rope whipping taut.

My own line strained. I braced, the fibers cutting into my hands. He surfaced ten feet downstream, clawing at the water, his mouth a round O of shock lost in the roar. The current had him in its grip, dragging him sideways, toward the lip where the water gathered itself for its final, plunging sigh.

Kaya was shouting from the far bank, her words shredded by the falls. I was hauling on the rope, but it was an angle, not a direct pull. I was dragging him toward me, but the river was dragging him down. The math was ugly and immediate. He wouldn’t make the ledge. He would arc out into the void, a piece of flotsam in the silver braid.

His head turned. His eyes met mine through the spray and the green glow of my goggles. I saw no plea there. Just a vast, resigned understanding. He had become a thing that broke worlds. It seemed fitting that a world would break him back.

Then, his hand went to his chest. Not to his pack, but to the stone beneath his soaked shirt.

A sound bloomed. Not the scream. This was a low, resonant *hum*, a single, pure note that did not compete with the falls but *threaded* it, like a wire of gold through ragged cloth. The air around Alex’s outstretched hand shimmered. Not with light, but with a coagulation of possibility. The spray droplets hung, became a lens, a matrix.

And the water… solidified.

It wasn’t ice. It was something else. A temporary, desperate architecture of reflected starlight and intent. A handhold, jagged and gleaming, erupted from the rushing surface beside him. A platform, like frosted glass, formed under his kicking feet for one crucial second.

He did not think. He acted. His hand slapped onto the glowing, crystalline hold. He pulled. He flung himself forward, one desperate lunge, as the constructed platform dissolved back into chaotic flow. His fingers scraped the edge of my ledge. I grabbed his wrist, my other hand locked on the safety line, and hauled him from the river’s maw.

We collapsed together on the narrow shelf, water sheeting off us, gasping in the ozone-scented, thunder-filled space. The hum was gone. The only evidence was a lingering, retinal afterimage of geometry, and the violent trembling of Alex’s body beside me.

He had not used the stone as a key. He had used it as a chisel, to fracture a moment of reality and wedge in a sliver of salvation. The cost was written in the new, deeper hollows of his face. He had traded a piece of his own substance for that purchase on light.

Kaya crossed in the aftermath, swift and sure as an otter. She saw our faces, the ghost of the constructed light still haunting the air. She said nothing. She simply took Alex’s pack, redistributing its weight to her own.

The ledge behind the falls was a treacherous, slimy gallery. We moved in single file, backs to the stone, faces to the roaring curtain. The world was this drumming vibration, this eternal, wet crash. It scoured thought clean. There was only the next footfall, the next handhold in the dark.

Then, the curtain thinned. The sound softened from a roar to a rumble, then to a murmur. We stepped out from behind the final fringe of falling water, and into the cavern mouth.

The silence was a shock. It was a velvet blanket, absorbing the last echoes of the river. We stood dripping on a smooth, sandy shore. Before us, the cavern opened like a cathedral’s nave, so vast our torch beams could not find its ceiling. And it was not dark.

It was alive with light.

A soft, blue-green bioluminescence clung to every surface. It dripped from stalactites in glowing rivulets. It carpeted the floor in a faint, pulsating moss. It clung to the walls in great, starry constellations of fungus. The light was cold, ancient, and utterly silent. It was the opposite of the frantic, electric blue of the drone’s LED or the Archon’s interface. This was a light that had never known a switch. It was a breath, a natural exhalation of the deep earth. The Throat’ light.

We walked into it, our own torchlight now an intrusion. The air was cool, damp, and carried a scent like wet stone and distant ozone. A wide, still river—an underground cousin to the one we’d left—flowed silently through the center of the cavern, its surface a perfect black mirror reflecting the bioluminescent ceiling. This was no mere cave. It was a geography. A hidden world.

We followed the riverbank, our footsteps hushed. The scale was humbling. Stone pillars, thick as redwoods, soared into the gloom, their flanks adorned with the living light. We were insects in a giant’s ossuary.

After a time, we found a dry shelf of stone above the waterline. We made camp there, a tiny island of human frailty in the vast, luminous dark. We did not light a fire. The living light was enough. We ate cold rations. The silence was a presence, watching us eat.

“You shaped it,” Kaya said finally, her voice low, respectful of the quiet. She was looking at Alex.

He stared at his hands, turning them over in the eerie glow. “It shaped me back.” His voice was ragged. “I didn’t… command it. I asked. And it took something to answer.”

“What?” I asked.

He looked up, and his eyes held the same cold, biological fire as the walls. “I don’t know yet. A memory? A possibility? It feels like… a vacancy.”

The technological anxiety was not gone. It had mutated. It wasn’t about the drone watching, or the Archon’s scan. It was about this symbiosis. Alex had become an interface himself. The stone wasn’t a tool in his pack. It was a graft. His use of it had opened a channel, and the price of the transaction was a piece of his own human data, overwritten by the stone’s ancient, alien protocols. The machine wasn’t in the canyon. It was in his marrow, speaking in the language of light and loss.

I took first watch, sitting with my back to a glowing pillar, looking out over the black river. The clean fatigue of physical labor was a distant dream. My bones ached with a new kind of weariness—the fatigue of witnessing a world become unglued. We had sought a hidden world to escape a surveilled one. But the hidden world was not passive. It was participatory. It asked for payment in a currency we did not understand.

Kaya’s question from the raft returned to me, echoing in the vast, silent space. *What does the Throat do?*

I watched a droplet of luminous water detach from a stalactite high above and fall, a slow, green star, into the black river. It vanished without a sound, without a ripple. The mirror surface swallowed it whole.

The hook was no longer just about the blue light ahead, or the people who might be chasing us. It was about the transaction. The cost. We had passed through the waterfall, into the glowing belly of the earth. But the true threshold we had crossed was inside Alex. He had traded a fragment of his self for a handhold of light. The Throat, it seemed, did not just warn or test.

It metabolized.

And we were now deep in its digestive tract, moving softly toward its beating, broadcasting heart. The silence around us was not an absence. It was the sound of something listening, waiting to see what we would offer up next.



---

# Chapter 7

## **CHAPTER 7**

The silence was not an absence. It was a held breath.

We moved in the faint, fungal glow, our footsteps swallowed by the soft silt of the cavern floor. The river had narrowed to a slick black ribbon on our right, a guidewire of obsidian. The pillars here were thinner, more numerous, like the petrified ribs of some colossal, extinct beast. Their light did not push the darkness back so much as stain it, turning the air a viscous, aqueous green. We were wading through a drowned forest of light.

Alex led, his movements precise, economical. He did not touch the stone at his chest, but I saw the way his head tilted, as if listening to a frequency just beyond the drip and sigh of the cavern. He was our compass now, tuned to a signal the rest of us were deaf to. Kael walked at his shoulder, a shadow with a hunter’s fluid patience, his eyes constantly parsing the darkness between the pillars. Sam and Kaya followed, their breaths the loudest things in the chamber.

It was Kaya who broke the quiet. Her voice was low, but it didn’t carry. It fell at her feet.

“The air tastes different.”

I realized she was right. The mineral dampness was gone. In its place was a scent like ozone after a lightning strike, and underneath it, something sweetly organic, like decaying fruit. It was the smell of a circuit frying. Of something ripening past its prime.

“Stay close,” Kael said, his words a gravelly echo. “The path is… soft.”

He meant the floor. The silt gave way to a substance like firm clay, but it shimmered with a faint, internal phosphorescence. Each step left a brief, ghostly footprint that faded after a second. We were walking on a membrane. A skin.

The first illusion was not a vision. It was a pressure.

It began as a tightening in my temples, a sense of weight against my sternum. Then came the sound—not a new sound, but a familiar one amplified, distorted. The whine of the drone. It wasn’t the single mechanical mosquito from the forest. It was a swarm. A cloud of them, buzzing just behind my ears, in the space between my skull and my brain. I stopped, my hand going to my knife.

“You hear that?” My voice was tight.

Kael glanced back, his face a mask of green shadow. “Hear what?”

The whine intensified, vibrating in my molars. I could see them then, not with my eyes, but in the periphery of my mind: a thousand black specks, like flies, hovering in the dark vaults above, their lenses focused on the back of my neck. The old contract, the respectful death in the forest—it was a naive fantasy here. There was no privacy. The Archon’s eye was not something you could outrun. It was a condition of the world. It was in the air.

“The drones,” I said, my breath coming short.

Alex turned. His eyes were not on me, but on the space around my head. He frowned. “There’s nothing there, Leo.”

“They’re *there*.” The conviction was a cold stone in my gut. The whine was a physical drill-bit. I could feel the heat of their tiny engines, the data-stream bleeding from my body into their receivers. My hunt was being catalogued, my grief digitized, my every private ritual broadcast on some cold, administrative screen.

Kaya stepped toward me, then flinched back as if hitting a wall. Her own face had gone pale. She wasn’t looking at me. She was staring at the black river. “No,” she whispered. “No, no, no.”

To her, the river was no longer still. It was churning, frothing. And things were rising from it. Pale, slender shapes, tangled in kelp and memory. Hands. Faces. The drowned from the inlet, their eyes milky with accusation, their mouths open in silent, water-clogged screams. They reached for her, their fingers brushing the silt of the bank.

“Kaya,” Sam said, reaching for her arm.

She jerked away from him, from the river only she could see. “Don’t you see them? They’re right there!”

Sam saw nothing but the placid obsidian ribbon. But his own illusion was descending. He stiffened, his academic curiosity sharpening into avarice. He looked past Kaya, past the river, to a space between two pillars. His glasses reflected not green light, but a brilliant, golden glow. “The library,” he breathed. “The complete archive. It’s… it’s all there. The lost codices. The untranslated core-logs. They’re just… waiting.”

He took a step off the path, toward the empty, glowing air.

“Sam, stop!” Kael’s command was a whip-crack, but it didn’t land. Sam was already gone, lured into a personal maze of scholarly glory.

The cavern was unraveling us. It was metabolizing our anxieties, our desires, and spitting them back as tangible reality. The pressure in my head swelled. The drone-swarm coalesced into a single, dark shape at the end of the passage—a humanoid silhouette, featureless, waiting. The Archon’s agent. It had been here all along. It had let us lead it right to the source.

I drew my knife. The blade caught the green light and held it, a shard of cold fire.
